(September 2016)">not in citation given</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Ottoman_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Ottoman Treaty">Anglo-Ottoman Treaty</a>, also known as the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Balta_Liman" title="Treaty of Balta Liman">Treaty of Balta Liman</a> that opened the Ottoman markets directly to English and French competitors, would be seen as one of the staging posts along this development.\n</p><p>By developing commercial centres and routes, encouraging people to extend the area of cultivated land in the country and international trade through its dominions, the state performed basic economic functions in the Empire. But in all this the financial and political interests of the state were dominant. Within the social and political system they were living in, Ottoman administrators could not have seen the desirability of the dynamics and principles of the capitalist and mercantile economies developing in Western Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Inalcik1970218_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Inalcik1970218-165">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the early 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Egypt" title="Ottoman Egypt">Ottoman Egypt</a> had an advanced economy, with a <a href="/wiki/Per-capita_income" class="mw-redirect" title="Per-capita income">per-capita income</a> comparable to that of leading <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western European</a> countries such as <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, and higher than the overall average income of <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-batou_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-batou-166">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Economic historian Jean Barou estimated that, in terms of 1960 dollars, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> in 1800 had a per-capita income of $232 ($1,025 in 1990 dollars). In comparison, per-capita income in terms of 1960 dollars for France in 1800 was $240 ($1,060 in 1990 dollars), for <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a> in 1800 was $177 ($782 in 1990 dollars), and for Japan in 1800 was $180 ($795 in 1990 dollars).<sup id="cite_ref-batou189_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-batou189-167">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Economic historian <a href="/wiki/Paul_Bairoch" title="Paul Bairoch">Paul Bairoch</a> argues that <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a> contributed to <a href="/wiki/Deindustrialization" title="Deindustrialization">deindustrialization</a> in the Ottoman Empire. In contrast to the <a href="/wiki/Protectionism" title="Protectionism">protectionism</a> of <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, Japan, and <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, the Ottoman Empire had a <a href="/wiki/Economic_liberalism" title="Economic liberalism">liberal trade</a> policy, open to foreign imports. This has origins in <a href="/wiki/Capitulations_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Capitulations of the Ottoman Empire">capitulations of the Ottoman Empire</a>, dating back to the first commercial treaties signed with France in 1536 and taken further with <a href="/wiki/Capitulation_(treaty)" title="Capitulation (treaty)">capitulations</a> in 1673 and 1740, which lowered <a href="/wiki/Duty_(economics)" title="Duty (economics)">duties</a> to 3% for imports and exports. The liberal Ottoman policies were praised by British economists such as <a href="/wiki/J._R._McCulloch" class="mw-redirect" title="J. R. McCulloch">J. R. McCulloch</a> in his <i>Dictionary of Commerce</i> (1834), but later criticized by British politicians such as <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli" title="Benjamin Disraeli">Benjamin Disraeli</a>, who cited the Ottoman Empire as "an instance of the injury done by unrestrained competition" in the 1846 <a href="/wiki/Corn_Laws" title="Corn Laws">Corn Laws</a> debate.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>There has been free trade in Turkey, and what has it produced? It has destroyed some of the finest manufactures of the world. As late as 1812 these manufactures existed; but they have been destroyed. That was the consequences of competition in Turkey, and its effects have been as pernicious as the effects of the contrary principle in Spain.\n</p></blockquote>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Demographics of the Ottoman Empire">Demographics of the Ottoman Empire</a></div>\n<p>A population estimate for the empire of 11,692,480 for the 1520\xe2\x80\x931535 period was obtained by counting the households in Ottoman tithe registers, and multiplying this number by 5.<sup id="cite_ref-Kabaday\xc4\xb1_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kabaday\xc4\xb1-170">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> For unclear reasons, the population in the 18th century was lower than that in the 16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> An estimate of 7,230,660 for the first census held in 1831 is considered a serious undercount, as this census was meant only to register possible conscripts.<sup id="cite_ref-Kabaday\xc4\xb1_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kabaday\xc4\xb1-170">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ottoman_Smyrna.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Ottoman_Smyrna.jpg/220px-Ottoman_Smyrna.jpg" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Ottoman_Smyrna.jpg/330px-Ottoman_Smyrna.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Ottoman_Smyrna.jpg/440px-Ottoman_Smyrna.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="750" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ottoman_Smyrna.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Smyrna" title="Smyrna">Smyrna</a> under Ottoman rule in 1900</div></div></div>\n<p>Censuses of Ottoman territories only began in the early 19th century. Figures from 1831 onwards are available as official census results, but the censuses did not cover the whole population. For example, the 1831 census only counted men and did not cover the whole empire.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinross1979281_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinross1979281-69">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kabaday\xc4\xb1_170-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kabaday\xc4\xb1-170">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> For earlier periods estimates of size and distribution of the population are based on observed demographic patterns.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, it began to rise to reach 25\xe2\x80\x9332 million by 1800, with around 10 million in the European provinces (primarily the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>), 11 million in the Asiatic provinces and around 3 million in the African provinces. Population densities were higher in the European provinces, double those in Anatolia, which in turn were triple the population densities of Iraq and <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> and five times the population density of Arabia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuataert2000110\xe2\x80\x93111_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuataert2000110\xe2\x80\x93111-173">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bridge_and_Galata_Area,_Istanbul,_Turkey_by_Abdullah_Fr%C3%A8res,_ca._1880-1893_(LOC).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Bridge_and_Galata_Area%2C_Istanbul%2C_Turkey_by_Abdullah_Fr%C3%A8res%2C_ca._1880-1893_%28LOC%29.jpg/220px-Bridge_and_Galata_Area%2C_Istanbul%2C_Turkey_by_Abdullah_Fr%C3%A8res%2C_ca._1880-1893_%28LOC%29.jpg" width="220" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Bridge_and_Galata_Area%2C_Istanbul%2C_Turkey_by_Abdullah_Fr%C3%A8res%2C_ca._1880-1893_%28LOC%29.jpg/330px-Bridge_and_Galata_Area%2C_Istanbul%2C_Turkey_by_Abdullah_Fr%C3%A8res%2C_ca._1880-1893_%28LOC%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Bridge_and_Galata_Area%2C_Istanbul%2C_Turkey_by_Abdullah_Fr%C3%A8res%2C_ca._1880-1893_%28LOC%29.jpg/440px-Bridge_and_Galata_Area%2C_Istanbul%2C_Turkey_by_Abdullah_Fr%C3%A8res%2C_ca._1880-1893_%28LOC%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="967" data-file-height="784" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bridge_and_Galata_Area,_Istanbul,_Turkey_by_Abdullah_Fr%C3%A8res,_ca._1880-1893_(LOC).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>View of <a href="/wiki/Galata" title="Galata">Galata</a> (<a href="/wiki/Karak%C3%B6y" title="Karak\xc3\xb6y">Karak\xc3\xb6y</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Galata_Bridge" title="Galata Bridge">Galata Bridge</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horn" title="Golden Horn">Golden Horn</a>, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1880\xe2\x80\x931893</span></div></div></div>\n<p>Towards the end of the empire\'s existence <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a> was 49 years, compared to the mid-twenties in Serbia at the beginning of the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuataert2000112_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuataert2000112-174">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> Epidemic diseases and <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a> caused major disruption and demographic changes. In 1785 around one sixth of the Egyptian population died from plague and Aleppo saw its population reduced by twenty percent in the 18th century. Six famines hit Egypt alone between 1687 and 1731 and the last famine to hit Anatolia was four decades later.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuataert2000113_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuataert2000113-175">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The rise of port cities saw the clustering of populations caused by the development of steamships and railroads. Urbanization increased from 1700 to 1922, with towns and cities growing. Improvements in health and sanitation made them more attractive to live and work in. Port cities like Salonica, in Greece, saw its population rise from 55,000 in 1800 to 160,000 in 1912 and \xc4\xb0zmir which had a population of 150,000 in 1800 grew to 300,000 by 1914.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuataert2000114_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuataert2000114-176">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> Some regions conversely had population falls \xe2\x80\x93 Belgrade saw its population drop from 25,000 to 8,000 mainly due to political strife.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuataert2000114_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuataert2000114-176">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Economic and political migrations made an impact across the empire. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian</a> and Austria-Habsburg annexation of the Crimean and Balkan regions respectively saw large influxes of Muslim refugees \xe2\x80\x93 200,000 Crimean Tartars fleeing to Dobruja.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuataert2000115_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuataert2000115-178">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> Between 1783 and 1913, approximately 5\xe2\x80\x937 million refugees flooded into the Ottoman Empire, at least 3.8 million of whom were from Russia. Some migrations left indelible marks such as political tension between parts of the empire (e.g. Turkey and Bulgaria) whereas centrifugal effects were noticed in other territories, simpler demographics emerging from diverse populations. Economies were also impacted with the loss of artisans, merchants, manufacturers and agriculturists.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuataert2000116_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuataert2000116-179">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Since the 19th century, a large proportion of Muslim peoples from the Balkans emigrated to present-day Turkey. These people are called <i><a href="/wiki/Muhacir" title="Muhacir">Muhacir</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> By the time the Ottoman Empire came to an end in 1922, half of the urban population of Turkey was descended from Muslim refugees from Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-books.google_b_99-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google_b-99">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Languages of the Ottoman Empire">Languages of the Ottoman Empire</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1911_Ottoman_Calendar.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/1911_Ottoman_Calendar.jpg/220px-1911_Ottoman_Calendar.jpg" width="220" height="376" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/1911_Ottoman_Calendar.jpg/330px-1911_Ottoman_Calendar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/1911_Ottoman_Calendar.jpg/440px-1911_Ottoman_Calendar.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="1026" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1911_Ottoman_Calendar.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1911 Ottoman calendar in <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turkish_language" title="Ottoman Turkish language">Ottoman Turkish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_language" title="Bulgarian language">Bulgarian</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Ottoman Turkish was the official language of the Empire. It was an <a href="/wiki/Oghuz_languages" title="Oghuz languages">Oghuz</a> <a href="/wiki/Turkic_languages" title="Turkic languages">Turkic language</a> highly influenced by <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>. The Ottomans had several influential languages: Turkish, spoken by the majority of the people in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> and by the majority of Muslims of the Balkans except in <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bosnia" class="mw-redirect" title="Bosnia">Bosnia</a>; Persian, only spoken by the educated;<sup id="cite_ref-Bertold_Spuler_page_69_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bertold_Spuler_page_69-181">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> Arabic, spoken mainly in <a href="/wiki/Arabia" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabia">Arabia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> and parts of the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Berber_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Berber language">Berber</a> in North Africa. In the last two centuries, usage of these became limited, though, and specific: Persian served mainly as a literary language for the educated,<sup id="cite_ref-Bertold_Spuler_page_69_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bertold_Spuler_page_69-181">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> was used for Islamic prayers.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a>, in its Ottoman variation, was a language of military and administration since the nascent days of the Ottomans. The Ottoman constitution of 1876 did officially cement the official imperial status of Turkish.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Because of a low literacy rate among the public (about 2\xe2\x80\x933% until the early 19th century and just about 15% at the end of the 19th century), ordinary people had to hire <a href="/wiki/Scribes" class="mw-redirect" title="Scribes">scribes</a> as "special request-writers" (<i>arzuh\xc3\xa2lci</i>s) to be able to communicate with the government.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> The ethnic groups continued to speak within their families and neighborhoods (<a href="/wiki/Mahalle" title="Mahalle">mahalles</a>) with their own languages (e.g., Jews, Greeks, Armenians, etc.). In villages where two or more populations lived together, the inhabitants would often speak each other\'s language. In cosmopolitan cities, people often spoke their family languages; many of those who were not ethnic <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">Turks</a> spoke Turkish as a second language.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="Unbalanced scales.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/45px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png" width="45" height="40" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/90px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="354" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span"><b>This section\'s representation of one or more viewpoints about a controversial issue may be unbalanced or inaccurate.</b><span class="hide-when-compact"> Please <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ottoman_Empire&amp;action=edit">improve the article</a> or discuss the issue on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Ottoman_Empire" title="Talk:Ottoman Empire">talk page</a>.</span>  <small><i>(November 2010)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg/220px-Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg" width="220" height="297" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg/330px-Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg/440px-Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1185" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Abd%C3%BClmecid_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Abd\xc3\xbclmecid II">Abd\xc3\xbclmecid II</a> was the last <a href="/wiki/Caliph" class="mw-redirect" title="Caliph">caliph</a> of Islam and a member of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_dynasty" title="Ottoman dynasty">Ottoman dynasty</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>In the Ottoman imperial system, even though there existed a hegemonic power of Muslim control over the non-Muslim populations, non-Muslim communities had been granted state recognition and protection in the Islamic tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-emigrnonm_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emigrnonm-185">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> The officially accepted state <a href="/wiki/D%C4%ABn" class="mw-redirect" title="D\xc4\xabn">D\xc4\xabn</a> <i>(<a href="/wiki/Madh%27hab" class="mw-redirect" title="Madh&#39;hab">Madh\'hab</a>)</i> of the Ottomans was <a href="/wiki/Sunni" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> <i>(<a href="/wiki/Hanafi_jurisprudence" class="mw-redirect" title="Hanafi jurisprudence">Hanafi jurisprudence</a>).</i><sup id="cite_ref-Gunduz_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gunduz-186">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Until the second half of the 15th century the empire had a Christian majority, under the rule of a Muslim minority.<sup id="cite_ref-Benton_109-110_146-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Benton_109-110-146">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> In the late 19th century, the non-Muslim population of the empire began to fall considerably, not only due to secession, but also because of migratory movements.<sup id="cite_ref-emigrnonm_185-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emigrnonm-185">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> The proportion of Muslims amounted to 60% in the 1820s, gradually increasing to 69% in the 1870s and then to 76% in the 1890s.<sup id="cite_ref-emigrnonm_185-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emigrnonm-185">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> By 1914, only 19.1% of the empire\'s population was non-Muslim, mostly made up of Jews and Christian Greeks, Assyrians, and Armenians.<sup id="cite_ref-emigrnonm_185-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emigrnonm-185">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Islam">Islam</span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Islam in the Ottoman Empire">Islam in the Ottoman Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Caliphate" title="Ottoman Caliphate">Ottoman Caliphate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_persecution_of_Alevis" title="Ottoman persecution of Alevis">Ottoman persecution of Alevis</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tile_with_Calligraphy.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Tile_with_Calligraphy.JPG/220px-Tile_with_Calligraphy.JPG" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Tile_with_Calligraphy.JPG/330px-Tile_with_Calligraphy.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Tile_with_Calligraphy.JPG/440px-Tile_with_Calligraphy.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2025" data-file-height="1584" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tile_with_Calligraphy.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Calligraphic" class="mw-redirect" title="Calligraphic">Calligraphic</a> writing on a <a href="/wiki/Fritware" title="Fritware">fritware</a> tile, depicting the names of <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_in_Islam" title="Muhammad in Islam">Muhammad</a> and the first <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">caliphs</a>, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1727</span><sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Turkic peoples practiced a variety of <a href="/wiki/Shamanism" title="Shamanism">shamanism</a> before adopting Islam. <a href="/wiki/Abbasid" class="mw-redirect" title="Abbasid">Abbasid</a> influence in Central Asia was ensured through a process that was greatly facilitated by the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Transoxiana" title="Muslim conquest of Transoxiana">Muslim conquest of Transoxiana</a>. Many of the various Turkic tribes\xe2\x80\x94including the <a href="/wiki/Oghuz_Turks" title="Oghuz Turks">Oghuz Turks</a>, who were the ancestors of both the Seljuks and the Ottomans\xe2\x80\x94gradually converted to Islam, and brought the religion with them to Anatolia beginning in the 11th century.\n</p><p>Muslim sects regarded as heretical, such as the <a href="/wiki/Druze" title="Druze">Druze</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ismailis" class="mw-redirect" title="Ismailis">Ismailis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alevi" class="mw-redirect" title="Alevi">Alevis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alawites" title="Alawites">Alawites</a>, ranked below Jews and Christians.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> In 1514, Sultan <a href="/wiki/Selim_I" title="Selim I">Selim I</a> ordered the massacre of 40,000 Anatolian <a href="/wiki/Alevi" class="mw-redirect" title="Alevi">Alevis</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Qizilbash" title="Qizilbash">Qizilbash</a></i>), whom he considered a <a href="/wiki/Fifth_column" title="Fifth column">fifth column</a> for the rival <a href="/wiki/Safavid" class="mw-redirect" title="Safavid">Safavid</a> empire. Selim was also responsible for an unprecedented and rapid expansion of the Ottoman Empire into the Middle East, especially through his <a href="/wiki/Ottoman%E2%80%93Mamluk_War_(1516%E2%80%9317)" title="Ottoman\xe2\x80\x93Mamluk War (1516\xe2\x80\x9317)">conquest of the entire Mamluk Sultanate of Egypt</a>. With these conquests, Selim further solidified the Ottoman claim for being an Islamic <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">caliphate</a>, although Ottoman sultans had been claiming the title of caliph since the 14th century starting with <a href="/wiki/Murad_I" title="Murad I">Murad I</a> (reigned 1362 to 1389).<sup id="cite_ref-Lambton_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lambton-5">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The caliphate would remain held by Ottoman sultans for the rest of the office\'s duration, which ended with its abolition on 3 March 1924 by the <a href="/wiki/Grand_National_Assembly_of_Turkey" title="Grand National Assembly of Turkey">Grand National Assembly of Turkey</a> and the exile of the last caliph, <a href="/wiki/Abd%C3%BClmecid_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Abd\xc3\xbclmecid II">Abd\xc3\xbclmecid II</a>, to France.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity_and_Judaism">Christianity and Judaism</span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Christianity in the Ottoman Empire">Christianity in the Ottoman Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="History of the Jews in the Ottoman Empire">History of the Jews in the Ottoman Empire</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gennadios_II_and_Mehmed_II.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Gennadios_II_and_Mehmed_II.jpg/180px-Gennadios_II_and_Mehmed_II.jpg" width="180" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/11/Gennadios_II_and_Mehmed_II.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="316" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gennadios_II_and_Mehmed_II.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mehmed_the_Conqueror" title="Mehmed the Conqueror">Mehmed the Conqueror</a> and Patriarch <a href="/wiki/Gennadius_Scholarius" title="Gennadius Scholarius">Gennadius II</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In the Ottoman Empire, in accordance with the Muslim <i><a href="/wiki/Dhimmi" title="Dhimmi">dhimmi</a></i> system, Christians were guaranteed limited freedoms (such as the right to worship). They were forbidden to carry weapons or ride on horseback, their houses could not overlook those of Muslims, in addition to various other legal limitations.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> Many Christians and Jews converted in order to secure full status in the society. Most, however, continued to practice their old religions without restriction.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Under the <a href="/wiki/Millet_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Millet (Ottoman Empire)">millet</a> system, non-Muslim people were considered subjects of the Empire, but were not subject to the Muslim faith or Muslim law. The Orthodox millet, for instance, was still officially legally subject to <a href="/wiki/Corpus_Juris_Civilis" title="Corpus Juris Civilis">Justinian\'s Code</a>, which had been in effect in the Byzantine Empire for 900 years. Also, as the largest group of non-Muslim subjects (or <i><a href="/wiki/Dhimmi" title="Dhimmi">zimmi</a></i>) of the Islamic Ottoman state, the Orthodox millet was granted a number of special privileges in the fields of politics and commerce, and had to pay higher taxes than Muslim subjects.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Similar millets were established for the Ottoman Jewish community, who were under the authority of the <i><a href="/wiki/Hakham_Bashi" title="Hakham Bashi">Haham Ba\xc5\x9f\xc4\xb1</a></i> or Ottoman <a href="/wiki/Chief_rabbi" class="mw-redirect" title="Chief rabbi">Chief rabbi</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Apostolic_Church" title="Armenian Apostolic Church">Armenian Orthodox</a> community, who were under the authority of a head bishop; and a number of other religious communities as well.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-193">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> Some argue that the millet system is an example of pre-modern <a href="/wiki/Religious_pluralism" title="Religious pluralism">religious pluralism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social-political-religious_structure">Social-political-religious structure</span></h3>\n<p>Society, government and religion was inter-related in complex ways after about 1800, in a complex overlapping, inefficient system that Atat\xc3\xbcrk systematically dismantled after 1922.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>  In Constantinople, the Sultan  ruled two distinct domains the secular government and the religious hierarchy. Religious officials formed the <a href="/wiki/Ulama" title="Ulama">Ulama</a> Who had control of religious teachings and theology, and also the Empire\'s judicial system, Giving them a major voice in day-to-day affairs in communities across the Empire (but not including the non-Moslem millets). They were powerful enough to reject the military reforms proposed by Sultan <a href="/wiki/Selim_III" title="Selim III">Selim III</a>. His successor Sultan <a href="/wiki/Mahmud_II" title="Mahmud II">Mahmud II</a> (r. 1808\xe2\x80\x931839) first won ulama approval before proposing similar reforms.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>  The secularization program of Atat\xc3\xbcrk brought ended the ulema and their institutions. The caliphate was abolished, madrasas were closed down and the sharia courts abolished.  He replaced the Arabic alphabet with Latin letters, ended the religious school system, and gave women some political rights.  Many rural traditionalists never accepted this secularization, and by the 1990s they were reasserting a demand for a larger role for Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The political system was transformed by the destruction of the <a href="/wiki/Janissaries" title="Janissaries">Janissaries</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Auspicious_Incident" title="Auspicious Incident">Auspicious Incident</a> of 1826. They were very powerful military/governmental/police force that revolted. Sultan <a href="/wiki/Mahmud_II" title="Mahmud II">Mahmud II</a> crushed the revolt, executed the leaders, and disbanded the large organization.  That set the stage for a slow process of modernization of government functions, as the government sought, with mixed success, to adopt the main elements of Western bureaucracy and military technology. The Janissaries had been recruited from Christians and other minorities; their abolition enabled the emergence of a Turkish elite to control the Ottoman Empire.  The problem was that the Turkish element was very poorly educated, lacking higher schools of any sort, and locked into a Turkish language that used Arabic alphabet that inhibited wider learning.  The large number of ethnic and religious minorities were tolerated in their own separate segregated domains called "<a href="/w/index.php?title=Milletts&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Milletts (page does not exist)">milletts</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> They were primarily Greek, Armenian or Jewish.  In each locality they governed themselves, spoke their own language, ran their own schools, cultural and religious institutions, and paid somewhat higher taxes. They had no power outside the millett. The Imperial government protected them, and prevented major violent clashes between ethnic groups. However the millets showed very little loyalty to the Empire. Ethnic nationalism, based on distinctive religion and language, provided a centripetal force that eventually destroyed the Ottoman Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> In addition, Muslim ethnic groups, which were not part of the millett system, especially the Arabs and the Kurds, were outside the Turkish culture and develop their own separate nationalism. The British sponsored Arab nationalism in the First World War, promising an independent Arab state in return for Arab support.  Most Arabs supported the Sultan but those near Mecca bought the British promise.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the local level, power was held beyond the control of the Sultan by the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Ayan" title="Ottoman Ayan">"ayan"</a> or local notables. The ayan collected taxes, formed local armies to compete with other notables, took a reactionary attitude toward political or economic change, and often defied policies handed down by the Sultan.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The economic system made little progress. Printing was forbidden until the 18th century,  for fear of defiling the secret documents of Islam. The millets, however, Were allowed their own presses, using Greek, Hebrew, Armenian and other languages that greatly facilitated nationalism. The religious prohibition on charging interest foreclosed most of the entrepreneurial skills among Muslims, although it did flourish among the Jews and Christians. \n</p><p>After the 18th century, the Ottoman Empire was clearly shrinking, as Russia put on heavy pressure and expanded to its south; Egypt became effectively independent in 1805 and the British later took it over, along with Cyprus. Greece became independent, and Serbia and other Balkan areas became highly restive is the force of nationalism pushed against imperialism.  The French took over Algeria and Tunisia.  Europeans all thought it was a sick man in rapid decline. Only the Germans seemed helpful, in their support led to the Empire joining the central powers in 1915, and coming out one of the heaviest losers of the First World War in 1918.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Culture of the Ottoman Empire">Culture of the Ottoman Empire</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WaldmeierLebanon.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/WaldmeierLebanon.gif/220px-WaldmeierLebanon.gif" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/WaldmeierLebanon.gif/330px-WaldmeierLebanon.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/WaldmeierLebanon.gif/440px-WaldmeierLebanon.gif 2x" data-file-width="948" data-file-height="706" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WaldmeierLebanon.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Depiction of a <a href="/wiki/Hookah" title="Hookah">hookah</a> shop in <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>, Ottoman Empire</div></div></div>\n<p>The Ottomans absorbed some of the traditions, art and institutions of cultures in the regions they conquered, and added new dimensions to them. Numerous traditions and cultural traits of previous empires (in fields such as architecture, cuisine, music, leisure and government) were adopted by the Ottoman Turks, who elaborated them into new forms, resulting in a new and distinctively Ottoman cultural identity. Despite newer added amalgamations, the Ottoman dynasty, like their predecessors in the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Rum" title="Sultanate of Rum">Sultanate of Rum</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Seljuk_Empire" title="Seljuk Empire">Seljuk Empire</a>, were thoroughly Persianised in their culture, language, habits and customs, and therefore the empire has been described as a <a href="/wiki/Persianate_society" title="Persianate society">Persianate</a> empire.<sup id="cite_ref-iranica_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iranica-204">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> Intercultural marriages also played a part in creating the characteristic Ottoman elite culture. When compared to the Turkish folk culture, the influence of these new cultures in creating the culture of the Ottoman elite was clear.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_%E2%80%A6trialsanderrors_-_Yeni_Cami_and_Emin%C3%B6n%C3%BC_bazaar,_Constantinople,_Turkey,_ca._1895.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Flickr_-_%E2%80%A6trialsanderrors_-_Yeni_Cami_and_Emin%C3%B6n%C3%BC_bazaar%2C_Constantinople%2C_Turkey%2C_ca._1895.jpg/220px-Flickr_-_%E2%80%A6trialsanderrors_-_Yeni_Cami_and_Emin%C3%B6n%C3%BC_bazaar%2C_Constantinople%2C_Turkey%2C_ca._1895.jpg"
