//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Flickr_-_%E2%80%A6trialsanderrors_-_Yeni_Cami_and_Emin%C3%B6n%C3%BC_bazaar%2C_Constantinople%2C_Turkey%2C_ca._1895.jpg/440px-Flickr_-_%E2%80%A6trialsanderrors_-_Yeni_Cami_and_Emin%C3%B6n%C3%BC_bazaar%2C_Constantinople%2C_Turkey%2C_ca._1895.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3512" data-file-height="2560" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_%E2%80%A6trialsanderrors_-_Yeni_Cami_and_Emin%C3%B6n%C3%BC_bazaar,_Constantinople,_Turkey,_ca._1895.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Yeni_Cami" class="mw-redirect" title="Yeni Cami">Yeni Cami</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emin%C3%B6n%C3%BC" title="Emin\xc3\xb6n\xc3\xbc">Emin\xc3\xb6n\xc3\xbc</a> bazaar, Constantinople, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1895</span></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Slavery_(Ottoman_Empire)" class="mw-redirect" title="Slavery (Ottoman Empire)">Slavery</a> was a part of Ottoman society,<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> with most slaves employed as domestic servants. Agricultural slavery, such as that which was widespread in the Americas, was relatively rare. Unlike systems of <a href="/wiki/Chattel_slavery" class="mw-redirect" title="Chattel slavery">chattel slavery</a>, slaves under Islamic law were not regarded as movable property, but maintained basic, though limited, rights. This gave them a degree of protection against abuse.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> Female slaves were still sold in the Empire as late as 1908.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> During the 19th century the Empire came under pressure from Western European countries to outlaw the practice. Policies developed by various Sultans throughout the 19th century attempted to curtail the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_slave_trade" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottoman slave trade">Ottoman slave trade</a> but, since slavery did have centuries of religious backing and sanction, they never directly abolished the institution outright.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-193">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Plague_(disease)" title="Plague (disease)">Plague</a> remained a major scourge in Ottoman society until the second quarter of the 19th century. "Between 1701 and 1750, 37 larger and smaller plague epidemics were recorded in Istanbul, and 31 between 1751 and 1801."<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_literature" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottoman literature">Ottoman literature</a></div>\n<p>The two primary streams of Ottoman written literature are poetry and <a href="/wiki/Prose" title="Prose">prose</a>. Poetry was by far the dominant stream. Until the 19th century, Ottoman prose did not contain any examples of fiction: there were no counterparts to, for instance, the European <a href="/wiki/Romance_(heroic_literature)" class="mw-redirect" title="Romance (heroic literature)">romance</a>, short story, or novel. Analogue genres did exist, though, in both <a href="/wiki/Turkish_folk_literature" title="Turkish folk literature">Turkish folk literature</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Divan_poetry" class="mw-redirect" title="Divan poetry">Divan poetry</a>.\n</p><p>Ottoman <a href="/wiki/Divan_poetry" class="mw-redirect" title="Divan poetry">Divan poetry</a> was a highly ritualized and symbolic art form. From the <a href="/wiki/Persian_poetry" class="mw-redirect" title="Persian poetry">Persian poetry</a> that largely inspired it, it inherited a wealth of symbols whose meanings and interrelationships\xe2\x80\x94both of similitude (\xd9\x85\xd8\xb1\xd8\xa7\xd8\xb9\xd8\xa7\xd8\xaa \xd9\x86\xd8\xb8\xd9\x8a\xd8\xb1 mura\'\xc3\xa2t-i naz\xc3\xaer / \xd8\xaa\xd9\x86\xd8\xa7\xd8\xb3\xd8\xa8 ten\xc3\xa2s\xc3\xbcb) and opposition (\xd8\xaa\xd8\xb6\xd8\xa7\xd8\xaf tez\xc3\xa2d) were more or less prescribed. Divan poetry was composed through the constant juxtaposition of many such images within a strict metrical framework, thus allowing numerous potential meanings to emerge. The vast majority of Divan poetry was <a href="/wiki/Lyric_poetry" title="Lyric poetry">lyric</a> in nature: either <a href="/wiki/Gazel" title="Gazel">gazels</a> (which make up the greatest part of the repertoire of the tradition), or kas\xc3\xaedes. There were, however, other common genres, most particularly the mesnev\xc3\xae, a kind of <a href="/wiki/Courtly_romance" class="mw-redirect" title="Courtly romance">verse romance</a> and thus a variety of <a href="/wiki/Narrative_poetry" title="Narrative poetry">narrative poetry</a>; the two most notable examples of this form are the <a href="/wiki/Leyli_and_Majnun" class="mw-redirect" title="Leyli and Majnun">Leyli and Majnun</a> of <a href="/wiki/Fuz%C3%BBl%C3%AE" title="Fuz\xc3\xbbl\xc3\xae">Fuz\xc3\xbbl\xc3\xae</a> and the <a href="/wiki/H%C3%BCsn_%C3%BC_A%C5%9Fk" title="H\xc3\xbcsn \xc3\xbc A\xc5\x9fk">H\xc3\xbcsn \xc3\xbc A\xc5\x9fk</a> of <a href="/w/index.php?title=%C5%9Eeyh_G%C3%A2lib&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="\xc5\x9eeyh G\xc3\xa2lib (page does not exist)">\xc5\x9eeyh G\xc3\xa2lib</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nedim_(divan_edb.%C5%9Fairi).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Nedim_%28divan_edb.%C5%9Fairi%29.JPG/220px-Nedim_%28divan_edb.%C5%9Fairi%29.JPG" width="220" height="248" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Nedim_%28divan_edb.%C5%9Fairi%29.JPG/330px-Nedim_%28divan_edb.%C5%9Fairi%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Nedim_%28divan_edb.%C5%9Fairi%29.JPG/440px-Nedim_%28divan_edb.%C5%9Fairi%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="603" data-file-height="680" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nedim_(divan_edb.%C5%9Fairi).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ahmet_Ned%C3%AEm_Efendi" class="mw-redirect" title="Ahmet Ned\xc3\xaem Efendi">Ahmet Ned\xc3\xaem Efendi</a>, one of the most celebrated Ottoman poets</div></div></div>\n<p>Until the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Prose_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Prose of the Ottoman Empire">Ottoman prose</a> did not develop to the extent that contemporary Divan poetry did. A large part of the reason for this was that much prose was expected to adhere to the rules of sec (\xd8\xb3\xd8\xac\xd8\xb9, also transliterated as seci), or <a href="/wiki/Rhymed_prose" title="Rhymed prose">rhymed prose</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> a type of writing descended from the Arabic <a href="/wiki/Saj%27" title="Saj&#39;">saj\'</a> and which prescribed that between each adjective and <a href="/wiki/Noun" title="Noun">noun</a> in a string of words, such as a sentence, there must be a <a href="/wiki/Rhyme" title="Rhyme">rhyme</a>. Nevertheless, there was a tradition of prose in the literature of the time, though exclusively non-fictional in nature. One apparent exception was <a href="/wiki/Muhayyel%C3%A2t" class="mw-redirect" title="Muhayyel\xc3\xa2t">Muhayyel\xc3\xa2t</a> ("Fancies") by <a href="/wiki/Giritli_Ali_Aziz_Efendi" title="Giritli Ali Aziz Efendi">Giritli Ali Aziz Efendi</a>, a collection of stories of the fantastic written in 1796, though not published until 1867. The first novel published in the Ottoman Empire was by an Armenian named <a href="/wiki/Vartan_Pasha" title="Vartan Pasha">Vartan Pasha</a>. Published in 1851, the novel was entitled The Story of Akabi (Turkish: Akabi Hikyayesi) and was written in Turkish but with <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a> script.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Due to historically close ties with France, <a href="/wiki/French_literature" title="French literature">French literature</a> came to constitute the major Western influence on Ottoman literature throughout the latter half of the 19th century. As a result, many of the same movements prevalent in France during this period also had their Ottoman equivalents: in the developing Ottoman prose tradition, for instance, the influence of <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romanticism</a> can be seen during the Tanzimat period, and that of the <a href="/wiki/Realism_(arts)" title="Realism (arts)">Realist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(literature)" title="Naturalism (literature)">Naturalist</a> movements in subsequent periods; in the poetic tradition, on the other hand, it was the influence of the <a href="/wiki/Symbolism_(arts)" title="Symbolism (arts)">Symbolist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parnassian_poets" class="mw-redirect" title="Parnassian poets">Parnassian</a> movements that became paramount.\n</p><p>Many of the writers in the Tanzimat period wrote in several different genres simultaneously: for instance, the poet <a href="/wiki/Nam%C4%B1k_Kemal" title="Nam\xc4\xb1k Kemal">Nam\xc4\xb1k Kemal</a> also wrote the important 1876 novel \xc4\xb0ntib\xc3\xa2h ("Awakening"), while the journalist <a href="/wiki/%C4%B0brahim_%C5%9Einasi" title="\xc4\xb0brahim \xc5\x9einasi">\xc4\xb0brahim \xc5\x9einasi</a> is noted for writing, in 1860, the first modern Turkish play, the <a href="/wiki/One_act_play" class="mw-redirect" title="One act play">one-act</a> comedy "\xc5\x9eair Evlenmesi" ("The Poet\'s Marriage"). An earlier play, a <a href="/wiki/Farce" title="Farce">farce</a> entitled "Vak\xc3\xa2yi\'-i \'Acibe ve Hav\xc3\xa2dis-i Garibe-yi Kef\xc5\x9fger Ahmed" ("The Strange Events and Bizarre Occurrences of the Cobbler Ahmed"), dates from the beginning of the 19th century, but there remains some doubt about its authenticity. In a similar vein, the novelist <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Midhat_Efendi" class="mw-redirect" title="Ahmed Midhat Efendi">Ahmed Midhat Efendi</a> wrote important novels in each of the major movements: Romanticism (Hasan Mell\xc3\xa2h y\xc3\xa2hud S\xc4\xb1rr \xc4\xb0\xc3\xa7inde Esr\xc3\xa2r, 1873; "Hasan the Sailor, or The Mystery Within the Mystery"), Realism (Hen\xc3\xbcz On Yedi Ya\xc5\x9f\xc4\xb1nda, 1881; "Just Seventeen Years Old"), and Naturalism (M\xc3\xbc\xc5\x9f\xc3\xa2hed\xc3\xa2t, 1891; "Observations"). This diversity was, in part, due to the Tanzimat writers\' wish to disseminate as much of the new literature as possible, in the hopes that it would contribute to a revitalization of Ottoman <a href="/wiki/Social_structure" title="Social structure">social structures</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_architecture" title="Ottoman architecture">Ottoman architecture</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dolmabah%C3%A7e_Palace_in_1862.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Dolmabah%C3%A7e_Palace_in_1862.jpg/220px-Dolmabah%C3%A7e_Palace_in_1862.jpg" width="220" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Dolmabah%C3%A7e_Palace_in_1862.jpg/330px-Dolmabah%C3%A7e_Palace_in_1862.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Dolmabah%C3%A7e_Palace_in_1862.jpg/440px-Dolmabah%C3%A7e_Palace_in_1862.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="801" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dolmabah%C3%A7e_Palace_in_1862.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Photo of the main entrance of <a href="/wiki/Dolmabah%C3%A7e_Palace" title="Dolmabah\xc3\xa7e Palace">Dolmabah\xc3\xa7e Palace</a> in 1862, taken by <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bedford_(photographer)" title="Francis Bedford (photographer)">Francis Bedford</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Ottoman_architecture" title="Ottoman architecture">Ottoman architecture</a> was influenced by <a href="/wiki/Persian_Architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Persian Architecture">Persian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_architecture" title="Byzantine architecture">Byzantine Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_architecture" title="Islamic architecture">Islamic</a> architectures. During the <a href="/wiki/Rise_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Rise of the Ottoman Empire">Rise period</a> the early or first Ottoman architecture period, Ottoman art was in search of new ideas. The <a href="/wiki/Growth_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Growth of the Ottoman Empire">growth period</a> of the Empire become the classical period of architecture, when Ottoman art was at its most confident. During the years of the <a href="/wiki/Stagnation_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Stagnation of the Ottoman Empire">Stagnation period</a>, Ottoman architecture moved away from this style, however.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bridge_on_the_Drina_July_2009.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Bridge_on_the_Drina_July_2009.jpg/220px-Bridge_on_the_Drina_July_2009.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Bridge_on_the_Drina_July_2009.jpg/330px-Bridge_on_the_Drina_July_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Bridge_on_the_Drina_July_2009.jpg/440px-Bridge_on_the_Drina_July_2009.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1900" data-file-height="1425" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bridge_on_the_Drina_July_2009.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mehmed_Pa%C5%A1a_Sokolovi%C4%87_Bridge" title="Mehmed Pa\xc5\xa1a Sokolovi\xc4\x87 Bridge">Mehmed Pa\xc5\xa1a Sokolovi\xc4\x87 Bridge</a>, completed in 1577 by <a href="/wiki/Mimar_Sinan" title="Mimar Sinan">Mimar Sinan</a>, the greatest architect of the classical period of Ottoman architecture</div></div></div>\n<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Tulip_Era_in_the_Ottoman_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Tulip Era in the Ottoman Empire">Tulip Era</a>, it was under the influence of the highly ornamented styles of Western Europe; <a href="/wiki/Baroque" title="Baroque">Baroque</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rococo" title="Rococo">Rococo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Empire_(style)" class="mw-redirect" title="Empire (style)">Empire</a> and other styles intermingled. Concepts of Ottoman architecture concentrate mainly on the <a href="/wiki/Mosque#Architecture" title="Mosque">mosque</a>. The mosque was integral to society, <a href="/wiki/Urban_planning" title="Urban planning">city planning</a> and communal life. Besides the mosque, it is also possible to find good examples of Ottoman architecture in <a href="/wiki/Soup_kitchen" title="Soup kitchen">soup kitchens</a>, theological schools, hospitals, <a href="/wiki/Turkish_bath" title="Turkish bath">Turkish baths</a> and tombs.\n</p><p>Examples of Ottoman architecture of the classical period, besides Istanbul and <a href="/wiki/Edirne" title="Edirne">Edirne</a>, can also be seen in Egypt, Eritrea, Tunisia, Algiers, the Balkans and Romania, where mosques, bridges, fountains and schools were built. The art of Ottoman decoration developed with a multitude of influences due to the wide ethnic range of the Ottoman Empire. The greatest of the court artists enriched the Ottoman Empire with many pluralistic artistic influences, such as mixing traditional <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_art" title="Byzantine art">Byzantine art</a> with elements of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_art" title="Chinese art">Chinese art</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decorative_arts">Decorative arts</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ottoman_miniature_painters.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Ottoman_miniature_painters.jpg/220px-Ottoman_miniature_painters.jpg" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Ottoman_miniature_painters.jpg/330px-Ottoman_miniature_painters.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Ottoman_miniature_painters.jpg/440px-Ottoman_miniature_painters.jpg 2x" data-file-width="460" data-file-height="364" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ottoman_miniature_painters.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ottoman miniature painters</div></div></div>\n<p>The tradition of <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_miniature" title="Ottoman miniature">Ottoman miniatures</a>, painted to illustrate manuscripts or used in dedicated albums, was heavily influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Persian_miniature" title="Persian miniature">Persian</a> art form, though it also included elements of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_art" title="Byzantine art">Byzantine</a> tradition of <a href="/wiki/Manuscript_illumination" class="mw-redirect" title="Manuscript illumination">illumination</a> and painting.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> A Greek academy of painters, the <i>Nakkashane-i-Rum</i>, was established in the <a href="/wiki/Topkapi_Palace" class="mw-redirect" title="Topkapi Palace">Topkapi Palace</a> in the 15th century, while early in the following century a similar Persian academy, the <i>Nakkashane-i-Irani</i>, was added.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ottoman_illumination" title="Ottoman illumination">Ottoman illumination</a> covers non-figurative painted or drawn decorative art in books or on sheets in <i><a href="/wiki/Muraqqa" title="Muraqqa">muraqqa</a></i> or albums, as opposed to the figurative images of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_miniature" title="Ottoman miniature">Ottoman miniature</a>. It was a part of the Ottoman Book Arts together with the Ottoman miniature (<i>taswir</i>), calligraphy (<i>hat</i>), <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calligraphy" title="Islamic calligraphy">Islamic calligraphy</a>, bookbinding (<i>cilt</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Paper_marbling" title="Paper marbling">paper marbling</a> (<i>ebru</i>). In the Ottoman Empire, <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">illuminated and illustrated manuscripts</a> were commissioned by the Sultan or the administrators of the court. In Topkapi Palace, these manuscripts were created by the artists working in <i>Nakkashane</i>, the atelier of the miniature and illumination artists. Both religious and non-religious books could be illuminated. Also sheets for albums <i>levha</i> consisted of illuminated calligraphy (<i>hat</i>) of <i><a href="/wiki/Tughra" title="Tughra">tughra</a></i>, religious texts, verses from poems or proverbs, and purely decorative drawings.\n</p><p>The art of carpet <a href="/wiki/Weaving" title="Weaving">weaving</a> was particularly significant in the Ottoman Empire, carpets having an immense importance both as decorative furnishings, rich in religious and other symbolism, and as a practical consideration, as it was customary to remove one\'s shoes in living quarters.<sup id="cite_ref-foroqhi_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-foroqhi-219">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> The weaving of such carpets originated in the <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomadic</a> cultures of central Asia (carpets being an easily transportable form of furnishing), and eventually spread to the settled societies of Anatolia. Turks used carpets, rugs and <a href="/wiki/Kilim" title="Kilim">kilims</a> not just on the floors of a room, but also as a hanging on walls and doorways, where they provided additional insulation. They were also commonly donated to <a href="/wiki/Mosques" class="mw-redirect" title="Mosques">mosques</a>, which often amassed large collections of them.<sup id="cite_ref-foroqhip153_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-foroqhip153-220">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music_and_performing_arts">Music and performing arts</span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Ottoman_classical_music" title="Ottoman classical music">Ottoman classical music</a> was an important part of the education of the Ottoman elite. A number of the Ottoman sultans were accomplished musicians and composers themselves, such as <a href="/wiki/Selim_III" title="Selim III">Selim III</a>, whose compositions are often still performed today. Ottoman classical music arose largely from a confluence of <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_music" title="Byzantine music">Byzantine music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenian_music" class="mw-redirect" title="Armenian music">Armenian music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabic_music" title="Arabic music">Arabic music</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Persian_traditional_music" title="Persian traditional music">Persian music</a>. Compositionally, it is organised around rhythmic units called <a href="/wiki/Usul_(music)" class="mw-redirect" title="Usul (music)">usul</a>, which are somewhat similar to <a href="/wiki/Metre_(music)" title="Metre (music)">meter</a> in Western music, and <a href="/wiki/Melody" title="Melody">melodic</a> units called <a href="/wiki/Makam" title="Makam">makam</a>, which bear some resemblance to Western <a href="/wiki/Musical_mode" class="mw-redirect" title="Musical mode">musical modes</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Musical_instrument" title="Musical instrument">instruments</a> used are a mixture of Anatolian and Central Asian instruments (the <a href="/wiki/Baglama" class="mw-redirect" title="Baglama">saz</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Baglama" class="mw-redirect" title="Baglama">ba\xc4\x9flama</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kemenche" title="Kemenche">kemence</a>), other Middle Eastern instruments (the <a href="/wiki/Oud" title="Oud">ud</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Tanbur" title="Tanbur">tanbur</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Qanun_(instrument)" title="Qanun (instrument)">kanun</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ney" title="Ney">ney</a>), and\xe2\x80\x94later in the tradition\xe2\x80\x94Western instruments (the violin and the piano). Because of a geographic and cultural divide between the capital and other areas, two broadly distinct styles of music arose in the Ottoman Empire: Ottoman classical music, and folk music. In the provinces, several different kinds of <a href="/wiki/Folk_music" title="Folk music">folk music</a> were created. The most dominant regions with their distinguished musical styles are: Balkan-Thracian T\xc3\xbcrk\xc3\xbcs, North-Eastern (<a href="/wiki/Laz_people" title="Laz people">Laz</a>) T\xc3\xbcrk\xc3\xbcs, Aegean T\xc3\xbcrk\xc3\xbcs, Central Anatolian T\xc3\xbcrk\xc3\xbcs, Eastern Anatolian T\xc3\xbcrk\xc3\xbcs, and Caucasian T\xc3\xbcrk\xc3\xbcs. Some of the distinctive styles were: <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_military_band" title="Ottoman military band">Janissary Music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roma_music" class="mw-redirect" title="Roma music">Roma music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belly_dance" title="Belly dance">Belly dance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkish_folk_music" title="Turkish folk music">Turkish folk music</a>.\n</p><p>The traditional <a href="/wiki/Shadow_play" title="Shadow play">shadow play</a> called <a href="/wiki/Karag%C3%B6z_and_Hacivat" title="Karag\xc3\xb6z and Hacivat">Karag\xc3\xb6z and Hacivat</a> was widespread throughout the Ottoman Empire and featured characters representing all of the major ethnic and social groups in that culture.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> It was performed by a single puppet master, who voiced all of the characters, and accompanied by <a href="/wiki/Tambourine" title="Tambourine">tambourine</a> (<i>def</i>). Its origins are obscure, deriving perhaps from an older Egyptian tradition, or possibly from an Asian source.\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Abdulaziz.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Abdulaziz.jpg/137px-Abdulaziz.jpg" width="137" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Abdulaziz.jpg/205px-Abdulaziz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Abdulaziz.jpg/274px-Abdulaziz.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2104" data-file-height="3072" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><a href="/wiki/Sultan_Abd%C3%BClaziz" class="mw-redirect" title="Sultan Abd\xc3\xbclaziz">Sultan Abd\xc3\xbclaziz</a> was also a music composer.\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Surname_171b.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Surname_171b.jpg/127px-Surname_171b.jpg" width="127" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Surname_171b.jpg/191px-Surname_171b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Surname_171b.jpg/255px-Surname_171b.jpg 2x" data-file-width="440" data-file-height="690" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Miniature from <i><a href="/wiki/Abdulcelil_Levni" title="Abdulcelil Levni">Surname-i Vehbi</a></i> showing the <a href="/wiki/Mehteran" class="mw-redirect" title="Mehteran">Mehteran</a>, the music band of the <a href="/wiki/Janissaries" title="Janissaries">Janissaries</a>\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:39px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Karagoz_figures.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Karagoz_figures.jpg/200px-Karagoz_figures.jpg" width="200" height="152" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Karagoz_figures.jpg/300px-Karagoz_figures.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Karagoz_figures.jpg/400px-Karagoz_figures.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="776" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>The shadow play <a href="/wiki/Karag%C3%B6z_and_Hacivat" title="Karag\xc3\xb6z and Hacivat">Karag\xc3\xb6z and Hacivat</a> was widespread throughout the Ottoman Empire.\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_cuisine" title="Ottoman cuisine">Ottoman cuisine</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Enjoying_Coffee_Pera_Museum_2_b.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Enjoying_Coffee_Pera_Museum_2_b.jpg/170px-Enjoying_Coffee_Pera_Museum_2_b.jpg" width="170" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Enjoying_Coffee_Pera_Museum_2_b.jpg/255px-Enjoying_Coffee_Pera_Museum_2_b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Enjoying_Coffee_Pera_Museum_2_b.jpg/340px-Enjoying_Coffee_Pera_Museum_2_b.jpg 2x" data-file-width="458" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Enjoying_Coffee_Pera_Museum_2_b.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Enjoying <a href="/wiki/Turkish_coffee" title="Turkish coffee">coffee</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Harem" title="Harem">harem</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fran%C3%A7ois-Marie_Rosset_-_Femmes_Turcs_turques_de_Serquin,_leur_mani%C3%A8re_de_faire_leur_pain_-_Syrie_-_1790.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Fran%C3%A7ois-Marie_Rosset_-_Femmes_Turcs_turques_de_Serquin%2C_leur_mani%C3%A8re_de_faire_leur_pain_-_Syrie_-_1790.jpg/170px-Fran%C3%A7ois-Marie_Rosset_-_Femmes_Turcs_turques_de_Serquin%2C_leur_mani%C3%A8re_de_faire_leur_pain_-_Syrie_-_1790.jpg" width="170" height="234" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Fran%C3%A7ois-Marie_Rosset_-_Femmes_Turcs_turques_de_Serquin%2C_leur_mani%C3%A8re_de_faire_leur_pain_-_Syrie_-_1790.jpg/255px-Fran%C3%A7ois-Marie_Rosset_-_Femmes_Turcs_turques_de_Serquin%2C_leur_mani%C3%A8re_de_faire_leur_pain_-_Syrie_-_1790.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Fran%C3%A7ois-Marie_Rosset_-_Femmes_Turcs_turques_de_Serquin%2C_leur_mani%C3%A8re_de_faire_leur_pain_-_Syrie_-_1790.jpg/340px-Fran%C3%A7ois-Marie_Rosset_-_Femmes_Turcs_turques_de_Serquin%2C_leur_mani%C3%A8re_de_faire_leur_pain_-_Syrie_-_1790.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2247" data-file-height="3092" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fran%C3%A7ois-Marie_Rosset_-_Femmes_Turcs_turques_de_Serquin,_leur_mani%C3%A8re_de_faire_leur_pain_-_Syrie_-_1790.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Turkish women baking bread, 1790</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Ottoman_cuisine" title="Ottoman cuisine">Ottoman cuisine</a> refers to the cuisine of the capital, <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>, and the regional capital cities, where the melting pot of cultures created a common cuisine that most of the population regardless of ethnicity shared. This diverse cuisine was honed in the Imperial Palace\'s kitchens by chefs brought from certain parts of the Empire to create and experiment with different ingredients. The creations of the Ottoman Palace\'s kitchens filtered to the population, for instance through <a href="/wiki/Ramadan" title="Ramadan">Ramadan</a> events, and through the cooking at the <a href="/wiki/Yal%C4%B1" title="Yal\xc4\xb1">Yal\xc4\xb1s</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Pasha" title="Pasha">Pashas</a>, and from there on spread to the rest of the population.\n</p><p>Much of the cuisine of former Ottoman territories today is descended from a shared Ottoman cuisine, especially <a href="/wiki/Turkish_cuisine" title="Turkish cuisine">Turkish</a>, and including <a href="/wiki/Greek_cuisine" title="Greek cuisine">Greek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Balkan_cuisine" title="Balkan cuisine">Balkan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenian_cuisine" title="Armenian cuisine">Armenian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Middle_Eastern_cuisine" title="Middle Eastern cuisine">Middle Eastern</a> cuisines.<sup id="cite_ref-Fragner_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fragner-223">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> Many common dishes in the region, descendants of the once-common Ottoman cuisine, include <a href="/wiki/Yogurt" title="Yogurt">yogurt</a>, <a href="/wiki/D%C3%B6ner_kebab" class="mw-redirect" title="D\xc3\xb6ner kebab">d\xc3\xb6ner kebab</a>/<a href="/wiki/Gyro_(food)" title="Gyro (food)">gyro</a>/<a href="/wiki/Shawarma" title="Shawarma">shawarma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cac%C4%B1k" class="mw-redirect" title="Cac\xc4\xb1k">cac\xc4\xb1k</a>/tzatziki, <a href="/wiki/Ayran" title="Ayran">ayran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pita" title="Pita">pita</a> bread, <a href="/wiki/Feta" title="Feta">feta</a> cheese, <a href="/wiki/Baklava" title="Baklava">baklava</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lahmacun" class="mw-redirect" title="Lahmacun">lahmacun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moussaka" title="Moussaka">moussaka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yuvarlak" class="mw-redirect" title="Yuvarlak">yuvarlak</a>, <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6fte" class="mw-redirect" title="K\xc3\xb6fte">k\xc3\xb6fte</a>/keft\xc3\xa9s/kofta, <a href="/wiki/B%C3%B6rek" title="B\xc3\xb6rek">b\xc3\xb6rek</a>/boureki, <a href="/wiki/Rak%C4%B1" title="Rak\xc4\xb1">rak\xc4\xb1</a>/<a href="/wiki/Rakia" title="Rakia">rakia</a>/<a href="/wiki/Tsipouro" title="Tsipouro">tsipouro</a>/<a href="/wiki/Tsikoudia" title="Tsikoudia">tsikoudia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meze" title="Meze">meze</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dolma" title="Dolma">dolma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sarma_(food)" title="Sarma (food)">sarma</a>, rice <a href="/wiki/Pilaf" title="Pilaf">pilaf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkish_coffee" title="Turkish coffee">Turkish coffee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sujuk" title="Sujuk">sujuk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kashk" title="Kashk">kashk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ke%C5%9Fkek" title="Ke\xc5\x9fkek">ke\xc5\x9fkek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manti_(dumpling)" class="mw-redirect" title="Manti (dumpling)">manti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lavash" title="Lavash">lavash</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kanafeh" title="Kanafeh">kanafeh</a>, and more.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_and_technology">Science and technology</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Science_and_technology_in_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Science and technology in the Ottoman Empire">Science and technology in the Ottoman Empire</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ottoman_Imperial_Museum_(Today-_Istanbul_Archaeology_Museums).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Ottoman_Imperial_Museum_%28Today-_Istanbul_Archaeology_Museums%29.jpg/220px-Ottoman_Imperial_Museum_%28Today-_Istanbul_Archaeology_Museums%29.jpg" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Ottoman_Imperial_Museum_%28Today-_Istanbul_Archaeology_Museums%29.jpg/330px-Ottoman_Imperial_Museum_%28Today-_Istanbul_Archaeology_Museums%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Ottoman_Imperial_Museum_%28Today-_Istanbul_Archaeology_Museums%29.jpg/440px-Ottoman_Imperial_Museum_%28Today-_Istanbul_Archaeology_Museums%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="736" data-file-height="569" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ottoman_Imperial_Museum_(Today-_Istanbul_Archaeology_Museums).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ottoman Imperial Museum, today the <a href="/wiki/Istanbul_Archaeology_Museums" class="mw-redirect" title="Istanbul Archaeology Museums">Istanbul Archaeology Museums</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Over the course of Ottoman history, the Ottomans managed to build a large collection of libraries complete with translations of books from other cultures, as well as original manuscripts.<sup id="cite_ref-\xc3\x81goston_and_Alan_Masters583_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-\xc3\x81goston_and_Alan_Masters583-48">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> A great part of this desire for local and foreign manuscripts arose in the 15th century. <a href="/wiki/Mehmet_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Mehmet II">Sultan Mehmet II</a> ordered <a href="/wiki/Georgios_Amiroutzes" class="mw-redirect" title="Georgios Amiroutzes">Georgios Amiroutzes</a>, a Greek scholar from <a href="/wiki/Trabzon" title="Trabzon">Trabzon</a>, to translate and make available to Ottoman educational institutions the geography book of <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>. Another example is <a href="/wiki/Ali_Qushji" title="Ali Qushji">Ali Qushji</a> \xe2\x80\x93 an <a href="/wiki/Islamic_astronomy" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic astronomy">astronomer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_mathematics" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic mathematics">mathematician</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_physics" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic physics">physicist</a> originally from <a href="/wiki/Samarkand" title="Samarkand">Samarkand</a> \xe2\x80\x93 who became a professor in two madrasas and influenced Ottoman circles as a result of his writings and the activities of his students, even though he only spent two or three years in Istanbul before his death.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Istambul_observatory_in_1577.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Istambul_observatory_in_1577.jpg/220px-Istambul_observatory_in_1577.jpg" width="220" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Istambul_observatory_in_1577.jpg/330px-Istambul_observatory_in_1577.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Istambul_observatory_in_1577.jpg/440px-Istambul_observatory_in_1577.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2356" data-file-height="2029" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Istambul_observatory_in_1577.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Istanbul_observatory_of_Taqi_ad-Din" class="mw-redirect" title="Istanbul observatory of Taqi ad-Din">Istanbul observatory of Taqi ad-Din</a> in 1577</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Taqi_al-Din_Muhammad_ibn_Ma%27ruf" class="mw-redirect" title="Taqi al-Din Muhammad ibn Ma&#39;ruf">Taqi al-Din</a> built the <a href="/wiki/Istanbul_observatory_of_Taqi_al-Din" class="mw-redirect" title="Istanbul observatory of Taqi al-Din">Istanbul observatory of Taqi al-Din</a> in 1577, where he carried out observations until 1580. He calculated the <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentricity</a> of the Sun\'s orbit and the annual motion of the <a href="/wiki/Apogee" class="mw-redirect" title="Apogee">apogee</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Tekeli_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tekeli-225">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> However, the observatory\'s primary purpose was almost certainly <a href="/wiki/Astrology" title="Astrology">astrological</a> rather than astronomical, leading to its destruction in 1580 due to the rise of a clerical faction that opposed its use for that purpose.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> He also experimented with <a href="/wiki/Steam_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Steam power">steam power</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Egypt" title="Ottoman Egypt">Ottoman Egypt</a> in 1551, when he invented a <a href="/wiki/Steam_jack" class="mw-redirect" title="Steam jack">steam jack</a> driven by a rudimentary <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1660 the Ottoman scholar <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ibrahim_Efendi_al-Zigetvari_Tezkireci&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ibrahim Efendi al-Zigetvari Tezkireci (page does not exist)">Ibrahim Efendi al-Zigetvari Tezkireci</a> translated <a href="/wiki/No%C3%ABl_Duret" title="No\xc3\xabl Duret">No\xc3\xabl Duret</a>\'s French astronomical work (written in 1637) into Arabic.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/%C5%9Eerafeddin_Sabuncuo%C4%9Flu" title="\xc5\x9eerafeddin Sabuncuo\xc4\x9flu">\xc5\x9eerafeddin Sabuncuo\xc4\x9flu</a> was the author of the first surgical atlas and the last major <a href="/wiki/Medicine_in_medieval_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Medicine in medieval Islam">medical encyclopedia from the Islamic world</a>. Though his work was largely based on <a href="/wiki/Abu_al-Qasim_al-Zahrawi" class="mw-redirect" title="Abu al-Qasim al-Zahrawi">Abu al-Qasim al-Zahrawi</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Al-Tasrif" title="Al-Tasrif">Al-Tasrif</a></i>, Sabuncuo\xc4\x9flu introduced many innovations of his own. Female surgeons were also illustrated for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>An example of a watch that measured time in minutes was created by an Ottoman watchmaker, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Meshur_Sheyh_Dede&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Meshur Sheyh Dede (page does not exist)">Meshur Sheyh Dede</a>, in 1702.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the early 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Egypt_under_Muhammad_Ali" class="mw-redirect" title="Egypt under Muhammad Ali">Egypt under Muhammad Ali</a> began using <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam engines</a> for industrial manufacturing, with industries such as <a href="/wiki/Ironworks" title="Ironworks">ironworks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Textile_manufacturing" title="Textile manufacturing">textile manufacturing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paper_mill" title="Paper mill">paper mills</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hulling" class="mw-redirect" title="Hulling">hulling</a> mills moving towards steam power.<sup id="cite_ref-batou193_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-batou193-231">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> Economic historian Jean Batou argues that the necessary economic conditions existed in Egypt for the adoption of <a href="/wiki/Oil" title="Oil">oil</a> as a potential energy source for its steam engines later in the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-batou193_231-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-batou193-231">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Ishak_Efendi" title="Ishak Efendi">Ishak Efendi</a> is credited with introducing the then current Western scientific ideas and developments to the Ottoman and wider Muslim world, as well as the invention of a suitable Turkish and Arabic scientific terminology, through his translations of Western works.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oil_wrestling_match_in_the_gardens_of_the_Sultan%27s_Palace.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Oil_wrestling_match_in_the_gardens_of_the_Sultan%27s_Palace.jpg/220px-Oil_wrestling_match_in_the_gardens_of_the_Sultan%27s_Palace.jpg" width="220" height="134" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Oil_wrestling_match_in_the_gardens_of_the_Sultan%27s_Palace.jpg/330px-Oil_wrestling_match_in_the_gardens_of_the_Sultan%27s_Palace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Oil_wrestling_match_in_the_gardens_of_the_Sultan%27s_Palace.jpg/440px-Oil_wrestling_match_in_the_gardens_of_the_Sultan%27s_Palace.jpg 2x" data-file-width="768" data-file-height="469" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oil_wrestling_match_in_the_gardens_of_the_Sultan%27s_Palace.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ottoman wrestlers in the gardens of <a href="/wiki/Topkap%C4%B1_Palace" title="Topkap\xc4\xb1 Palace">Topkap\xc4\xb1 Palace</a>, in the 19th century</div></div></div>\n<p>The main sports Ottomans were engaged in were <a href="/wiki/Turkish_wrestling" class="mw-redirect" title="Turkish wrestling">Turkish wrestling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hunting" title="Hunting">hunting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkish_archery" title="Turkish archery">Turkish archery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horseback_riding" class="mw-redirect" title="Horseback riding">horseback riding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cirit" class="mw-redirect" title="Cirit">equestrian javelin throw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arm_wrestling" title="Arm wrestling">arm wrestling</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Swimming_(sport)" title="Swimming (sport)">swimming</a>. European model sports clubs were formed with the spreading popularity of <a href="/wiki/Football" title="Football">football</a> matches in 19th century <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>. The leading clubs, according to timeline, were <a href="/wiki/Be%C5%9Fikta%C5%9F_J.K." class="mw-redirect" title="Be\xc5\x9fikta\xc5\x9f J.K.">Be\xc5\x9fikta\xc5\x9f Gymnastics Club</a> (1903), <a href="/wiki/Galatasaray_S.K." title="Galatasaray S.K.">Galatasaray Sports Club</a> (1905), <a href="/wiki/Fenerbah%C3%A7e_S.K." title="Fenerbah\xc3\xa7e S.K.">Fenerbah\xc3\xa7e Sports Club</a> (1907), <a href="/wiki/MKE_Ankarag%C3%BCc%C3%BC" title="MKE Ankarag\xc3\xbcc\xc3\xbc">MKE Ankarag\xc3\xbcc\xc3\xbc (formerly Turan Sanatkarag\xc3\xbcc\xc3\xbc)</a> (1910) in <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>. Football clubs were formed in other provinces too, such as <a href="/wiki/Kar%C5%9F%C4%B1yaka_S.K." title="Kar\xc5\x9f\xc4\xb1yaka S.K.">Kar\xc5\x9f\xc4\xb1yaka Sports Club</a> (1912), <a href="/wiki/Altay_S.K." title="Altay S.K.">Altay Sports Club</a> (1914) and <a href="/wiki/%C3%9Clk%C3%BCspor" title="\xc3\x9clk\xc3\xbcspor">Turkish Fatherland Football Club</a> (later <a href="/wiki/%C3%9Clk%C3%BCspor" title="\xc3\x9clk\xc3\xbcspor">\xc3\x9clk\xc3\xbcspor</a>) (1914) of <a href="/wiki/%C4%B0zmir" title="\xc4\xb0zmir">\xc4\xb0zmir</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span Tunisia</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Superpower#Superpowers_of_the_past" title="Superpower">Superpowers of the past</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Abolition_of_the_Ottoman_sultanate" title="Abolition of the Ottoman sultanate">The sultanate was abolished</a> on 1 November 1922 and the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Turkey" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic of Turkey">Republic of Turkey</a> was established on 29 October 1923. For further information, see <a href="/wiki/Defeat_and_dissolution_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Defeat and dissolution of the Ottoman Empire">Defeat and dissolution of the Ottoman Empire</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In Ottoman Turkish, the city was known by various names, among which were <i><a href="/wiki/Kostantiniyye" class="mw-redirect" title="Kostantiniyye">Kostantiniyye</a></i> (<span lang="ota-Arab" dir="rtl" title="Ottoman Turkish language text">\xd9\x82\xd8\xb3\xd8\xb7\xd9\x86\xd8\xb7\xd9\x8a\xd9\x86\xd9\x8a\xd9\x87</span>&#8206;) (replacing the suffix <i>-polis</i> with the Arabic <a href="/wiki/Nisba_(suffix)" class="mw-redirect" title="Nisba (suffix)">nisba</a>), <i><a href="/wiki/Dersaadet" class="mw-redirect" title="Dersaadet">Dersaadet</a></i> (<span lang="ota-Arab" dir="rtl" title="Ottoman Turkish language text">\xd8\xaf\xd8\xb1 \xd8\xb3\xd8\xb9\xd8\xa7\xd8\xaf\xd8\xaa</span>&#8206;) and <i>Istanbul</i> (<span lang="ota-Arab" dir="rtl" title="Ottoman Turkish language text">\xd8\xa7\xd8\xb3\xd8\xaa\xd8\xa7\xd9\x86\xd8\xa8\xd9\x88\xd9\x84</span>&#8206;). Names other than Istanbul gradually became obsolete in Turkish, and after Turkey\'s transition to Latin script in 1928, Istanbul become widely accepted internationally.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The sultan from 1512 to 1520.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Mehmed_VI" title="Mehmed VI">Mehmed VI</a>, the last Sultan, was expelled from Constantinople on 17 November 1922.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_S%C3%A8vres" title="Treaty of S\xc3\xa8vres">Treaty of S\xc3\xa8vres</a> (10 August 1920) afforded a small existence to the Ottoman Empire. On 1 November 1922, the <a href="/wiki/Grand_National_Assembly_of_Turkey" title="Grand National Assembly of Turkey">Grand National Assembly</a> (GNAT) abolished the sultanate and declared that all the deeds of the Ottoman regime in Istanbul were null and void as of 16 March 1920, the date of the <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Constantinople" title="Occupation of Constantinople">occupation of Constantinople</a> under the terms of the Treaty of S\xc3\xa8vres. The international recognition of the GNAT and the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_the_Grand_National_Assembly" title="Government of the Grand National Assembly">Government of Ankara</a> was achieved through the signing of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lausanne_(1923)" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Lausanne (1923)">Treaty of Lausanne</a> on 24 July 1923. The Grand National Assembly of Turkey promulgated the Republic on 29 October 1923, which ended the Ottoman Empire in history.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The empire also temporarily gained authority over distant overseas lands through declarations of allegiance to the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottoman Dynasty">Ottoman Sultan and Caliph</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_expedition_to_Aceh" title="Ottoman expedition to Aceh">declaration by the Sultan of Aceh</a> in 1565, or through temporary acquisitions of islands such as <a href="/wiki/Lanzarote" title="Lanzarote">Lanzarote</a> in the Atlantic Ocean in 1585, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.dzkk.tsk.tr/turkce/tarihimiras/AtlantikteTurkDenizciligi.php">Turkish Navy Official Website: "Atlantik\'te T\xc3\xbcrk Denizcili\xc4\x9fi"</a></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-40">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A lock-hold on trade between western Europe and Asia is often cited as a primary motivation for <a href="/wiki/Isabella_I_of_Castile" title="Isabella I of Castile">Isabella I of Castile</a> to fund <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a>\'s westward journey to find a sailing route to Asia and, more generally, for European seafaring nations to explore alternative trade routes (e.g. K. D. Madan, <i>Life and travels of Vasco Da Gama</i> (1998), 9; I. Stavans, <i>Imagining Columbus: the literary voyage</i> (2001), 5; W.B. Wheeler and S. Becker, <i>Discovering the American Past. A Look at the Evidence: to 1877</i> (2006), 105). This traditional viewpoint has been attacked as unfounded in an influential article by A.H. Lybyer ("The Ottoman Turks and the Routes of Oriental Trade", <i>English Historical Review</i>, 120 (1915), 577\xe2\x80\x93588), who sees the rise of Ottoman power and the beginnings of Portuguese and Spanish explorations as unrelated events. His view has not been universally accepted (cf. K.M. Setton, <i>The Papacy and the Levant (1204\xe2\x80\x931571), Vol. 2: The Fifteenth Century (Memoirs of the American Philosophical Society, Vol. 127)</i> (1978), 335).</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-Shaw-13-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Shaw-13_2-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Stanford Shaw, <i>History of the Ottoman Empire and Modern Turkey</i> (Cambridge: University Press, 1976), vol. 1 p. 13</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-auto-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-auto_3-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-auto_3-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">"In 1363 the Ottoman capital moved from Bursa to Edirne, although Bursa retained its spiritual and economic importance." <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.kultur.gov.tr/\xe2\x80\x93EN,33810/ottoman-capital-bursa.html"><i>Ottoman Capital Bursa</i></a>. Cambridge University Press. p.&#160;9. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-107-41144-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-107-41144-9">978-1-107-41144-9</a>. <q>Ottomanist historians have produced several works in the last decades, revising the traditional understanding of this period from various angles, some of which were not even considered as topics of historical inquiry in the mid-twentieth century. Thanks to these works, the conventional narrative of Ottoman history \xe2\x80\x93 that in the late sixteenth century the Ottoman Empire entered a prolonged period of decline marked by steadily increasing military decay and institutional corruption \xe2\x80\x93 has been discarded.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Second+Ottoman+Empire%3A+Political+and+Social+Transformation+in+the+Early+Modern+Period&amp;rft.pages=9&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-107-41144-9&amp;rft.aulast=Tezcan&amp;rft.aufirst=Baki&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AOttoman+Empire" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></li>\n<li><cite class="citation book">Woodhead, Christine (2011). "Crisis and Change, 1590\xe2\x80\x931699".  In \xc4\xb0nalc\xc4\xb1k, Halil; Donald Quataert. <i>An Economic and Social History of the Ottoman Empire, 1300\xe2\x80\x931914</i>. <b>2</b>. Cambridge University Press. p.&#160;553. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-521-57456-0" title="Special:BookSources/0-521-57456-0">0-521-57456-0</a>. <q>In the past fifty years, scholars have frequently tended to view this decreasing participation of the sultan in political life as evidence for \xe2\x80\x9cOttoman decadence,\xe2\x80\x9d which supposedly began at some time during the second half of the sixteenth century. But recently, more note has been taken of the fact that the Ottoman Empire was still a formidable military and political power throughout the seventeenth century, and that noticeable though limited economic recovery followed the crisis of the years around 1600; after the crisis of the 1683\xe2\x80\x9399 war, there followed a longer and more decisive economic upswing. Major evidence of decline was not visible before the second half of the eighteenth century.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Crisis+and+Change%2C+1590%E2%80%931699&amp;rft.btitle=An+Economic+and+Social+History+of+the+Ottoman+Empire%2C+1300%E2%80%931914&amp;rft.pages=553&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1994&amp;rft.isbn=0-521-57456-0&amp;rft.aulast=Faroqhi&amp;rft.aufirst=Suraiya&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AOttoman+Empire" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></li></ul>\n</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-AksanOW-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-AksanOW_22-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-AksanOW_22-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Aksan, Virginia (2007). <i>Ottoman Wars, 1700\xe2\x80\x931860: An Empire Besieged</i>.
